    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="51234abc8e844a238153d3ffebbcb2e5">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '5af593d7-2995-4504-a688-a087fdbaaac4'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:15000614598@163.com"><span>15000614598@163.com</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:zwfu@fudan.edu.cn"><span>zwfu@fudan.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Fu/Zhengwen">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>
      </div>
   </div>
   <div class="epub-sections"><div class="epub-section"><span class="epub-state">First published: </span><span class="epub-date">22 April 2024</span></div>

Sodium-Ion Batteries link" href="https://doi.org/10.1002/adfm.202403841">https://doi.org/10.1002/adfm.202403841</a></div><div class="epub-section cited-by-count"><span>Citations: <a    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d212906352" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Hard carbon (HC) with low cost and high specific capacity is considered the appropriate anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), but the low initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) caused by solid electrolyte interface (SEI) formation and inherent active defects impede its practical battery application. Here, the practicability of solid-state electrochemical (SSE) pre-sodiation technique for hard carbon anode is assessed to conquer such challenges. The uniformly pre-sodiated HC (Pre-HC) can be fabricated through the SSE reaction between the HC and preloaded sodium metal film without introducing a liquid electrolyte. After being immerged in the electrolyte, a thin artificial SEI with abundant inorganic species is formed on the surface of Pre-HC due to the spontaneous chemical reaction, and the ICE of HC is improved from 76.0% to 107.9%. Full cell paired with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode exhibits a high ICE of 94.0% with 70% of energy density augment (from 126.5 to 214.4&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) after anode pre-sodiation. Pre-HC anode still retains the pre-sodiation capacity of 671.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sub>Na</sub><sup>−1</sup> after being stored in the dry air for 2&nbsp;h. This work demonstrates the practical applicability of this SSE pre-sodiation strategy to enhance the energy density of SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202403841-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202403841-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Secondary batteries with high energy density and long cycle life have drawn extensive attention to the application of electrochemical energy storage systems (ESS) due to the increasing utilization of renewable energy such as wind and solar.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0001_R_d212906234e396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Chem.">1</a>, <a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0002_R_d212906234e396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - O. Guillon, Sustain. Energy Fuels">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), with high energy density and low self-discharge merits, have been widely used in 3C electronic devices and electric vehicles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0003_R_d212906234e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Cent. Sci.">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0004_R_d212906234e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Power Sources">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, state-of-the-art LIBs are unsuitable for grid-scale energy storage because of the limited reserve, uneven distribution, and high cost of lithium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0005_R_d212906234e406" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On account of the similar chemical properties of Na to Li, together with the wide distribution and low cost of sodium minerals, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been regarded as one of the most promising alternatives to LIBs for ESS.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0006_R_d212906234e412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Environ. Sci.">6</a>, <a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0007_R_d212906234e412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, graphite as a widely used anode in LIBs cannot be applied in SIBs due to the instability of low-stage graphite intercalation compounds (NaC<sub>x</sub>) and sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0008_R_d212906234e422" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0009_R_d212906234e422" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Carbon Energy">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thanks to the tremendous efforts of researchers, numerous intercalation-type anodes (Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>6</sub>O<sub>13</sub>, soft carbon, graphene oxide, etc.), alloy-type anodes (Ge, Sn, P, Sb, etc.) and conversion-type anodes (FeF<sub>3</sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub>, etc.) have been explored in the last decades.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0010_R_d212906234e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. – A Eur. J.">10</a>, <a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0011_R_d212906234e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Small">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hard carbon (HC) seems to be the optimal anode for commercial SIBs due to its low cost, environmental friendliness, and moderate balance between specific capacity and cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0012_R_d212906234e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Environ. Sci.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the low operation voltage (0.01–1.0&nbsp;V vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) of HC promotes the realization of high-energy-density SIBs when paired with appropriate cathode materials. Nevertheless, one bottleneck that hinders its practical application is the low initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) due to the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) and irreversible Na<sup>+</sup> trapped in the defects/voids/pores of the HC matrix. This initial capacity loss inevitably decreases the energy density of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0013_R_d212906234e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Carbon Energy">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Although various strategies, including intrinsic structure engineering, surface modification, and electrolyte optimization have been explored to improve the ICE of HC, the ICE is still restricted below 90%, with more than 10% capacity loss during the first cycle.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0014_R_d212906234e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Energy">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0015_R_d212906234e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, cathode and anode pre-sodiation have been regarded as effective strategies to compensate for the initial capacity loss.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0016_R_d212906234e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Lab">16</a>-<a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0018_R_d212906234e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Environ. Sci.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the cathode side, a series of sodium-rich sacrificial additives have been developed, such as Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0019_R_d212906234e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>3</sub>P,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0020_R_d212906234e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Commun.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaN<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0021_R_d212906234e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Electrochem. Commun.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0022_R_d212906234e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>4</sub>O<sub>4</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0023_R_d212906234e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Electrochim. Acta">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> these additives are introduced into cathode side, subsequently are decomposed into Na<sup>+</sup> and by-products. The generated extra Na<sup>+</sup> can serve for SEI formation and irreversible side reactions. However, issues including the toxicities of Na<sub>3</sub>P and NaN<sub>3</sub>, gas productions, and low pre-sodiation capacities (&lt;400&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, and Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>4</sub>O<sub>4</sub> inevitably hinder their practical application. Anode pre-sodiation by partially pre-storing Na into the anode is more effective since no harmful by-products exist. Electrochemical pre-sodiation by precycling anodes in the half-cell can improve the ICE of anodes effectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0024_R_d212906234e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - RSC. Adv.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the complex half-cell assembling and disassembling procedures are incompatible with practical applications. Recently, ≈100% ICE has been achieved by pre-sodiation strategies including direct contact between sodium metal and anode in liquid electrolytes or chemical reactions between anode and sodium biphenyl solution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0025_R_d212906234e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0026_R_d212906234e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, it is still a great challenge to control the ICE accurately by these methods due to the fast reaction rate between anode and sodium metal or sodium biphenyl solution. Besides, introducing electrolyte, biphenyl, and ether solvents in these methods inevitably increases the pre-sodiation cost.</p>
         
         <p>To overcome the limitations of the pre-sodiation strategies mentioned above, a facile and effective solid-state electrochemical (SSE) anode pre-sodiation (without liquid electrolyte or organic solution) strategy is proposed as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0001">1a</a>. Sodium metal film with the desired thickness is deposited on the HC anode surface by vacuum thermal evaporation, which can precisely control the pre-sodiation degree and avoid the danger of metallic sodium. Na<sub>2</sub>O forms between sodium and HC as the product of the chemical reaction between sodium and O<sub>2</sub> that HC adsorbs, which provides an ionic channel for Na<sup>+</sup> transport,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0027_R_d212906234e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Power Sources">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while electron transport required for electrochemical pre-sodiation is conducted at the direct contact between the Na metal and HC anode. Under the driving force of the chemical potential difference between HC and sodium metal, the HC anode can be pre-sodiated easily as the result of SSE reactions. The electrochemical performance of half-cells and full cells (against Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>) in two-electrode and three-electrode setups were measured and discussed to comprehensively understand the effects of SSE pre-sodiation and evaluate the practicability of this pre-sodiation technique. The air stability of the Pre-HC anode is also investigated as it is one critical challenge for the large-scale application of the anode pre-sodiation strategy. We believe this SSE pre-sodiation technique, with the merits of process compatibility, high pre-sodiation capacity, and accuracy, is a practical strategy to improve energy density and accelerate the industrialization of SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403841-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/00f29459-5d91-41ed-a17a-0e60413ab09f/adfm202403841-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/00f29459-5d91-41ed-a17a-0e60413ab09f/adfm202403841-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a1e434e0-5d12-443e-ac8e-d2f9e8858eb9/adfm202403841-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/00f29459-5d91-41ed-a17a-0e60413ab09f/adfm202403841-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403841-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403841" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The schematic illustration of SSE pre-sodiation of HC electrode. SEM and corresponding EDS images of b) the pristine HC and pre-sodiated HC (Pre-HC) anodes after aging for c,d) 2&nbsp;h and e,f) 72&nbsp;h under Ar ambient. Optical microscope images of g) sodium metal film @ Al foil, h) Pre-HC-2&nbsp;h, i) Pre-HC-24&nbsp;h, j) Pre-HC-72&nbsp;h, and k) pristine HC anodes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202403841-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202403841-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>In order to observe the changes during the solid-state electrochemical (SSE) pre-sodiation process, the morphologies of the pristine HC and pre-sodiated HC (Pre-HC) anodes after being covered by sodium metal with several aging times were first examined by SEM and EDS as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0001">1b–f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), the pristine HC anode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0001">1b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information) shows a typically microspheric structure with the size of 3–10&nbsp;µm, and the C element mapping matches well with the HC particles. After being covered by sodium metal, the HC anode was transferred to the glove box to prepare samples for SEM characterization, which took ≈2&nbsp;h. Pre-HC-2&nbsp;h (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0001">1c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a>, Supporting Information) shows short dendritic morphology and the nearly disappeared C element relative to Na element intensity on the mapping image. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0001">1d</a>, the Na element distribution in the depth direction of Pre-HC-2&nbsp;h presents an obvious segmentation of Na and C elements. These images support that the sodium metal thin film deposited by vacuum thermal evaporation remains on the HC surface within 2&nbsp;h of aging.</p>
         
         <p>With the increase of aging time, the dendritic Na disappears in Pre-HC-72&nbsp;h (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0001">1e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c</a>, Supporting Information) with the emergence of HC microspheres, and the C element relative intensity to Na element intensity from EDS mapping image becomes stronger with the gradually clear particle contours. It proves that the sodium metal on the HC surface can percolate through the HC anode just by SSE reaction without the participation of the liquid electrolyte. In addition, it can be observed from the cross-sectional profile (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0001">1f</a>) that the boundary of Na and C elements disappears. In contrast, the Na and C elements are distributed together in the HC electrode. These results demonstrate that the HC anode can be pre-sodiated well by solid diffusion of Na without introducing liquid electrolyte. This SSE pre-sodiation process is also tracked by the optical microscope and camera. After being covered by sodium metal, both the Al foil (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0001">1g</a>) and Pre-HC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0001">1h</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1e</a>, Supporting Information) present a grey color, while the color of Pre-HC gets darker after aging for 24&nbsp;h (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0001">1i</a>), and Pre-HC-72&nbsp;h (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0001">1j</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1f</a>, Supporting Information) shows brown color which is similar to the pristine HC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0001">1k</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1d</a>, Supporting Information) anode, indicating the successful SSE penetration of sodium metal into HC anode.</p>
         
         <p>This SSE pre-sodiation process is also characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD), Raman spectroscopy, and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) to track the changes in components as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0002">2</a>. The phase structure of HC powder was examined via XRD (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0002">2a</a>), two broad diffraction peaks at 23.3° and 43.5° can be well assigned to the (002) and (100) planes of HC. The XRD patterns of Pre-HC electrodes with different aging times are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0002">2b</a>. The (110) diffraction peak of sodium metal at 29.5° can be observed in Pre-HC-2&nbsp;h,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0028_R_d212906234e687" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Power Sources">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while the intensity of this peak gradually decreases over aging times, and there is no new peak appears in Pre-HC-72&nbsp;h, it means that sodium metal thin film on the surface of HC anode reacts with HC and sodium ions intercalate into HC particles with the formation of sodiated HC. The diffraction intensity from sodiated HC may be relatively weaker than that of sealing tape and thus cannot be detected by XRD.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403841-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/21ace216-ef44-48c8-b12f-0d859a2ef13f/adfm202403841-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/21ace216-ef44-48c8-b12f-0d859a2ef13f/adfm202403841-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/624fc7cd-2ab1-4c29-a2b8-d99230fc51c2/adfm202403841-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/21ace216-ef44-48c8-b12f-0d859a2ef13f/adfm202403841-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403841-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403841" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XRD patterns of a) HC powder and b) Pre-HC electrodes over aging times. c) Raman spectra of the pristine HC and Pre-HC electrodes over aging times. d) C 1s, e) O 1s, and f) Na 1s XPS spectra of the pristine HC and Pre-HC electrodes over aging times.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Raman spectra of pristine HC and Pre-HC electrodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0002">2c</a>) show two peaks at 1317 and 1600&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, arising from the C─C vibrations of disordered and graphitized carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0029_R_d212906234e720" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This result is consistent with the XRD mentioned above. The pristine HC shows a high I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio (1.39) due to a large number of defects in the HC matrix. After being covered by Na film and aging for 2&nbsp;h, the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio reduces to 1.10 in Pre-HC, which means Na is absorbed into the defects in HC and impedes the vibrations of disordered carbon with the decreased intensity of D-band,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0030_R_d212906234e734" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Energy Chem.">30</a>, <a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0031_R_d212906234e734" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> thus lower I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratios are observed in Pre-HC electrodes, with the increase of aging time, the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio gradually increases to 1.22 and 1.34 for Pre-HC-24&nbsp;h and Pre-HC-72&nbsp;h, this increase of I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio should be related to the recovery of the vibration of disordered carbon, which means the reduction of surface Na<sup>+</sup> absorbed by hard carbon defects due to more uniform diffusion of Na within HC matrix.</p>
         
         <p>Furthermore, the surface chemical composition of HC during pre-sodiation is also investigated by XPS. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0002">2d</a>, four peaks at 284.8, 286.0, 288.5, and 291.0&nbsp;eV in C 1s spectra of the pristine HC electrode correspond to C─C, C─O, C = O bonds from HC and C─F bond from PVDF binder, respectively. Among them, C─O and C = O should be assigned to the surface chemisorbed oxygen and CO<sub>2</sub> from the air onto HC. Compared with pristine HC, a new peak at 289.5&nbsp;eV from C 1s spectra of Pre-HC can be found, which belongs to C─O─Na and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. The O 1s spectra of the pristine HC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0002">2e</a>) show two peaks at 531.5 and 533.1&nbsp;eV from C─O and C = O, while Pre-HC anodes show four additional peaks at 530.8, 531.1, 532.0 and 536.4&nbsp;eV, corresponding to Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>O, C─O─Na and Na auger peaks, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0032_R_d212906234e777" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chemie – Int. Ed.">32</a>, <a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0033_R_d212906234e777" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>2</sub>O and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> originate from the chemical reaction between sodium and CO<sub>2</sub> and O<sub>2</sub> adsorbed by HC and in air during the sample transfer process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0034_R_d212906234e793" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peak at 289.5&nbsp;eV from C 1s spectra and the peak at 532.0&nbsp;eV from O 1s spectra of Pre-HC could be well assigned to C─O─Na as the pre-sodiation product,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0035_R_d212906234e799" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Electron Spectros. Relat. Phenomena">35</a>, <a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0036_R_d212906234e799" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Small">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> indicating the typical surface composition of pre-sodiated HC. The Na<sub>2</sub>O products may serve as sodium ion channels (≈10<sup>−12</sup>&nbsp;S&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) for SSE reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0027_R_d212906234e811" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Power Sources">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the C─C signal from HC appears at 284.8&nbsp;eV in the C 1s spectra of the Pre-HC anode, indicating an electronic pathway, thus constructing a perfect SSE reaction of metal sodium with HC. Therefore, sodium metal thin films on the surface of the HC anode could be embedded into the HC anode spontaneously through electrochemical reactions. In the Na 1s spectra of Pre-HC anodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0002">2f</a>), there are several apparent peaks at 1070.7, 1071.1, 1071.5, and 1072.5&nbsp;eV, and they correspond to Na metal, C─O─Na, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, and Na<sub>2</sub>O, respectively. Significantly, with the increase of aging time, the disappearance of the sodium metal peak in Na 1s spectra and the rise of the C─O─Na peak can be observed. This result is consistent with the morphology and color changes from SEM/EDS, optical images, XRD, and Raman data. It is convictive that the HC anode can be pre-sodiated thoroughly with sodium metal just by SSE reactions.</p>
         
         <p>According to previous studies, the open circuit potential of Pre-HC is usually lower than the redox potential of liquid electrolyte, so a SEI layer can be formed spontaneously on the surface of Pre-HC after being immersed in liquid electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0026_R_d212906234e828" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">26</a>, <a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0030_R_d212906234e828" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Energy Chem.">30</a>, <a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0032_R_d212906234e828" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chemie – Int. Ed.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Here, to examine the structure and chemical composition of HC and Pre-HC, both the pristine HC and Pre-HC-72&nbsp;h electrodes were immersed in liquid electrolyte (1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> EC/DMC = 1/1 + 5% FEC) for 24&nbsp;h and then observed by transmission electron microscopy (TEM). The high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images of pristine HC (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0003">3a,b</a>) show typical characters of graphite-like layers with interlayer distance of 0.382&nbsp;nm and disordered carbon regions. This structure is maintained in Pre-HC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0003">3c,d</a>), which means the SSE pre-sodiation process has no harmful effects on the structure of HC. Besides, a mixed nanocrystal layer with fringes belonging to the (200) and (220) lattice plane of NaF, (310) lattice plane of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and (200) lattice plane of Na<sub>2</sub>O can be observed on the Pre-HC surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0003">3e–h</a>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0037_R_d212906234e857" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> accompanied by the homogenously distributed C, Na, O and F elements on the Pre-HC surface from energy dispersive spectroscopy mapping images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), this preformed inorganic-rich layer on the HC surface may mainly come from the reduction of NaPF<sub>6</sub> and carbonate solvents as the internal layer of SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0009_R_d212906234e867" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Carbon Energy">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403841-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2a6bb8a1-832e-427c-9d13-514452fdb792/adfm202403841-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2a6bb8a1-832e-427c-9d13-514452fdb792/adfm202403841-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/53f9e454-4053-4536-a451-268570a5dbcb/adfm202403841-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2a6bb8a1-832e-427c-9d13-514452fdb792/adfm202403841-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403841-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403841" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images of a,b) the pristine HC, and c–h) Pre-HC after being immersed into the liquid electrolyte. i) C 1s, j) O 1s, and k) F 1s XPS spectra of the pristine HC and Pre-HC with 1&nbsp;µm Na in thickness after being immersed into the liquid electrolyte.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The chemical composition of this SEI layer and pre-sodiated products of HC were further investigated by XPS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0003">3i–k</a>). Except for the Na auger peak at 536.7&nbsp;eV in O 1s spectra,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0030_R_d212906234e896" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Energy Chem.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the small NaF signal generated by NaPF<sub>6</sub> hydrolysis and P─F bonds from NaPF<sub>6</sub> or Na<sub>x</sub>PO<sub>y</sub>F<sub>z</sub> adsorbed by HC in the F 1s spectra,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0038_R_d212906234e912" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">38</a>, <a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0039_R_d212906234e912" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Mater. Sci.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the composition of the pristine HC electrode in the XPS data has almost no change before and after immersed into the electrolyte. For the Pre-HC anode after being immersed into the liquid electrolyte, C 1s spectra peak at 289.7&nbsp;eV, and O 1s spectra peak at 530.5 and 531.7&nbsp;eV belong to C─O─Na and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. O 1s XPS peaks at 530.8&nbsp;eV could be indexed to Na<sub>2</sub>O. Compared to the Pre-HC anode before being immersed into the liquid electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0002">2d–f</a>), the relative proportions of these components, such as Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>O, and C─O─Na are changed, implying the new production of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>O, most of which may come from the reduction of carbonate solvents. Three peaks at 684.4, 687.0, and 688.5&nbsp;eV can be assigned to NaF, P─F, and C─F bonds in both the pristine HC and Pre-HC electrodes, while the relative intensity of NaF peak to C─F peak in F 1s spectra of the Pre-HC is much higher than that of the pristine HC, indicating that the chemical reaction of Pre-HC with the liquid electrolyte produces an SEI layer on its surface, where NaF is one of the inorganic components in SEI. Combined with the analysis from TEM, a stout SEI layer consisting of NaF, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, and Na<sub>2</sub>O inorganic components is formed spontaneously after being immersed into the liquid electrolyte, along with C─O─Na species as the pre-sodiation product on the Pre-HC anode. These results suggest that the SSE pre-sodiation of the HC electrode is beneficial for forming a stout SEI layer spontaneously with the liquid electrolyte, which should play an essential role in the stability of the anode/electrolyte interface for the long cycling performance of SIBs.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">To investigate the relationship between electrochemical performance of Pre-HC with the increased pre-loaded sodium contents, pristine HC and Pre-HC with 1 and 2.3&nbsp;µm sodium metal thin films (account for 14% and 32% of the discharge capacity of pristine HC, respectively) were tested in half-cells at 0.05&nbsp;C, Na anodes with aforementioned two thicknesses were deposited on Al foils and tested in half-cells to determine the capacity of extra Na ions (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0004">4a</a> shows the initial charge/discharge curves of pristine HC and Pre-HC. The specific discharge/charge capacities of pristine HC are 292.8/222.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with an ICE of 76%, which means 70.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> of irreversible capacity loss for the first cycle in pristine HC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting information). After pre-sodiation, the initial specific discharge capacities of pre-sodiated HC anodes with sodium thin film of 1&nbsp;µm (Abbreviated as HC-1&nbsp;µm Na) and of 2.3&nbsp;µm (Abbreviated as HC-2.3&nbsp;µm Na) decrease to 251.3 and 206.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, while the initial specific charge capacities of Pre-HC anodes remain unchanged. Thus, ICEs increase to 87.1% for HC-1&nbsp;µm Na and 107.9% for HC-2.3&nbsp;µm Na (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0004">4b</a>), and the irreversible capacity reduces to 32.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for HC-1&nbsp;µm Na. Furthermore, HC-2.3&nbsp;µm Na shows eliminated irreversible capacity with an additional capacity of 16.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. In addition, the open circuit voltage (OCV) drops are also recorded in reflection of pre-sodiation degrees (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403841-fig-0004">4c</a>). Compared with the pristine HC vs. Na half-cell, which shows a relatively high OCV of 2.1&nbsp;V, the OCVs of half-cells with HC-1&nbsp;µm Na and HC-2.3&nbsp;µm Na anodes reduce to 0.90 and 0.56&nbsp;V, respectively. This OCV drop tendency is in accordance with the results reported by other anode pre-sodiation strategies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0026_R_d212906234e979" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">26</a>, <a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0031_R_d212906234e979" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a>-<a href="#adfm202403841-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403841-bib-0033_R_d212906234e979" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The corresponding pre-sodiation efficiency (PE, the utilization rate of Na metal) values are calculated by the following formula, more than 90% of PE values are achieved for Pre-HC anodes, suggesting the high utilization rates of Na metal by SSE pre-sodiation.

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202403841-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{PE\ }} = \frac{{the\ eliminated\ irreversible\ capacit{{y}_{{\mathrm{pre}} - {\mathrm{sodiation}}}}\ }}{{the\ capacity\ of\ extra\ introduced\ Na\ metal}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
